tool
1	"""PyTorch reference for a Kimi-Linear W4A16 hybrid decode unit (batch=1).
2	
3	Correctness oracle, NOT the fast baseline (baseline.py) or the SOTA ceiling.
4	One repeating motif of Kimi-Linear-48B-A3B -- 3 KDA (gated-delta linear attn)
5	layers + 1 MLA (multi-head latent attention) layer, each + a 64-expert MoE FFN
6	-- decoded one token at a time, with the big projection weights stored as
7	**W4A16** (int4 weights, group-128 asymmetric, bf16 accumulation; the AWQ/GPTQ
8	format that open-source inference actually ships).
9	
10	Why W4A16 and not fp8: at batch-1 decode the bottleneck is *weight memory
11	traffic*. int4 weights are 0.25x the bytes of bf16 (4x less traffic), and a
12	fused dequant-GEMV realizes that win in the memory-bound regime where decode
… +360 more lines

tool
1	"""Optimized-PyTorch W4A16 baseline for the Kimi-Linear decode unit.
2	
3	The floor the solution must beat -- NOT the oracle (reference.py). Same int4
4	weights, same math, written the competent pure-PyTorch way: bf16 accumulation
5	and batched MoE expert dequant instead of the reference's fp32 per-expert loop.
6	
7	It still *materializes* each dequantized bf16 weight before the matmul (int4
8	read + bf16 write + bf16 read = ~9x the traffic of a fused path). That is the
9	whole point left on the table: stock PyTorch cannot fuse the int4 unpack into
10	the GEMV, so beating this baseline requires a custom fused dequant-GEMV kernel.
11	`score = baseline_latency / solution_latency`.
12	"""

tool
1	"""Correctness runner for the Kimi-Linear hybrid decode unit.
2	
3	Per-step correctness, not a chained trajectory: for each seed and a couple of
4	context lengths, the reference and the solution are given an IDENTICAL fed
5	state and one new token, each runs ONE decode step, and we compare the
6	next-token hidden plus the updated decode state (KDA recurrent state, MLA latent
7	cache) by cosine similarity. Cosine sim is robust to bf16/fp8 precision but
8	still rejects wrong answers. Chaining steps would just measure how fast two
9	precisions diverge, which is not a kernel-correctness signal.
10	"""
11	from __future__ import annotations

Let me understand the task fully: 1. I need to write solution.py exposing a `Model` class with `step(hidden, state) -> (hidden, state)`. 2. The step must be a SINGLE GPU kernel launch (Triton or CUDA via load_inline). 3. The weights are W4A16 quantized (int4, group-128 asymmetric). 4. Batch-1 decode, bandwidth-bound. 5. 4 layers [KDA, KDA, KDA, MLA] each followed by MoE FFN. Let me think about the computational structure per step: **Per layer:** - RMSNorm on hidden (2304) - Attention (KDA or MLA) - Residual add - RMSNorm - MoE (router + top-8 of 64 experts + 1 shared) - Residual add **KDA layer:** - q = q_proj(x): (2304,) @ dequant(2304x4096) → 4096 - k = k_proj(x): 2304x4096 - v = v_proj(x): 2304x4096 - g = g_proj(x): 2304x4096 - beta = beta_proj(x): 2304x32 bf16 - conv: q,k,v each get causal depthwise conv (kernel 4) on the 4096 channels + silu - Per head (32 heads, 128 dim): - q = q * scale (0.0884) - g = -softplus(g); exp decay - beta sigmoid - S[h] (128x128): S = S * exp(g)[:, None]; pred = S^T k; S += beta * k (v - pred)^T; o = S^T q... wait let me re-read. ```python S = st["S"] * g.exp()[:, :, None] # S[h,i,j] *= exp(g[h,i]) (decay along key-dim rows) pred = (S * k[:, :, None]).sum(1) # pred[h,j] = sum_i S[h,i,j]*k[h,i] = S^T k S = S + beta[:, None, None] * k[:, :, None] * (v - pred)[:, None, :] # S[h,i,j] += beta[h]*k[h,i]*(v[h,j]-pred[h,j]) o = (S * q[:, :, None]).sum(1) # o[h,j] = sum_i S[h,i,j]*q[h,i] = S^T q st["S"] = S return self.o_proj(o.reshape(H*Dk).to(bf16)) ``` So S is H=32 × 128 × 128 = 524288 fp32 elems = 2 MB per KDA layer. ×3 layers = 6MB state read/write per step. That's significant bandwidth but small vs weights. **MLA layer:** - q = q_proj(x): (2304,) @ (2304 × 32*192=6144) → 6144 - kv = kv_a(x): 2304 × 576 (512 c_kv + 64 k_rope) → 576 - rope on q_rope part and k_rope with position pos = current cache length (before append) - append c_kv, k_rope to caches (L+1) - kvb = kv_b(c_kv_all): (L+1, 512) @ (512 × 32*256=8192) → (L+1, 32, 256). This is the BIG one at long context: L×512×8192. Wait, this materializes per-token k_nope and v by multiplying the latent cache by kv_b. At ctx=16384, that's 16384 × 512 × 8192 = 68.7 GFLOP per step. Hmm, that's huge. Actually let me compute: 2*16384*512*8192 = 137 GFLOP. On an RTX PRO 6000 (Blackwell, ~126 TFLOPs bf16? probably more like 200-400 TFLOPS), that takes maybe 0.5-1ms. Hmm, but that's only if you DON'T do the absorb trick. Alternative (absorb): compute scores directly against the latent without materializing k_nope: - scores[l,h] = q_nope[h] · (c_kv[l] @ W_uk[h]) + q_rope[h] · k_rope[l] where kv_b output columns = H*(qk_nope + v_head), viewed as (H, 256), first 128 = k_nope per head, second 128 = v per head. W_k[h] is (512, 128) for head h; W_v[h] is (512,128). score[l,h] = (q_nope[h] · W_k[h]) · c_kv[l] * scale + q_rope·k_rope. So we can precompute q_latent[h] = q_nope[h] @ W_k[h] (128 @ 512x128 → 512-dim) per head... that's q_nope(h) 128-dim vector times W_k[h] (512x128): q_latent[h,j] = sum_d q_nope[h,d] * W_k[h][j,d]. That's 32 × 512 × 128 = 2M MACs = 4 MFLOP, tiny. Then scores = q_latent @ c_kv^T + ... - Then o[h] = sum_l p[l,h] * (c_kv[l] @ W_v[h]). Can compute as: first compute o_latent[h] = sum_l p[l,h] * c_kv[l] (512-dim), then o[h] = o_latent[h] @ W_v[h] (512→128). That avoids materializing the (L,8192) kvb matrix entirely! This is the classic MLA "absorbed" decode. FLOPs for attention: 2*(L*512*32) for scores + 2*(L*512*32) for weighted sum = 4*L*512*32 = 67M MACs at L=16k = 134 MFLOP. vs 137 GFLOP for the naive path. 1000x fewer FLOPs at long context! And bandwidth: absorbed attention reads c_kv (L×512 bf16 = 16.8MB at L=16384... wait 16384*512*2 = 16.8 MB) and k_rope (16384*64*2 = 2MB). Plus kv_b weight (512×8192 int4 = 2MB packed + scales... 512/128=4 groups ×8192 ×2 bytes ×2 (scales+zeros) = 4*8192*2*2=131KB). So ~19MB for attention at 16k context. Total at 16k: ≈ 232.6 + 12 + 19 ≈ 264MB per step. At 1.8 TB/s, minimum time ≈ 147µs. That's the ideal; we probably get 70-80% bandwidth efficiency → ~200µs. Baseline comparison: baseline dequantizes all weights → materializes bf16. Its traffic per step ≈ int4 read + bf16 write + bf16 read = 1× + 4× + 4× = 9× the int4 bytes ≈ 2.1GB per step... plus at 16k context, the naive MLA materialization computes kvb = c_kv(16384×512) @ kv_b(512×8192) = 137 GFLOP and reads kv_b as bf16 (materialized 512×8192×2 = 8.4MB) and writes kvb (16384×8192×2 = 268MB) then reads it. That's enormous. The baseline will be really slow at 16k. Probably ~10-20ms per token. So the speedup could be 50-100x at long context. Actually wait — the MoE expert dequant in the baseline: it dequants only the selected 8 experts (idx from topk). Good. But still materializes bf16 for them. Also note baseline's MLA: `kvb = (st["c_kv"] @ _wbf(self.kv_b))` — full (L,8192) materialization. At L=2048: 2048×8192×2×2 traffic (write+read) = 67MB extra, plus 8.4MB kv_b weight in bf16. At 16384: 537MB extra traffic + compute. OK. Now what's the practical plan? **The hard requirement: ONE kernel launch per step.** This is the megakernel constraint. Everything fused into a single Triton kernel (or CUDA kernel via load_inline). This is very hard because there are sequential dependencies across layers. Within a single kernel launch, we need grid-wide synchronization between stages. Options: 1. **Persistent kernel with cooperative-groups-style grid sync**: In CUDA, use `cooperative groups grid sync` — requires launching with cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel. With Triton, there's no grid sync... but you can do manual spin-wait barriers via atomics (grid-wide barrier with atomic counters — risky but doable if all blocks are resident; need persistent kernel sized to GPU's SM count to guarantee co-residency). 2. **One fat Triton kernel with a single program** doing everything sequentially in a loop over layers with... no, single program = single CTA, too slow to cover 230MB of bandwidth? Actually with enough threads per CTA a single CTA can pull a lot of bandwidth but nowhere near 1.8TB/s (a single SM sustains maybe ~30-60 GB/s). Need ~50-100+ CTAs concurrently (SM120 has... RTX PRO 6000 has 188 SMs? Let me check: RTX PRO 6000 Blackwell has 188 SMs, 96GB. Actually the RTX PRO 6000 Blackwell Server Edition: 188 SMs, slightly less than B200. Boost clock ~2.6GHz. Memory 1.8TB/s via GDDR7. Need lots of CTAs.) So the design: **persistent kernel, fixed grid = #SMs (or 2×SMs), with grid-wide sync implemented via atomic spin barriers between stages**. Each stage is a parallel-for over work items (e.g., output columns of GEMV) distributed across CTAs. Between stages, a grid barrier. Layers are sequential: rmsnorm → attention GEMVs → conv → KDA state update → o_proj → residual → rmsnorm → MoE router → expert GEMVs → down → residual → next layer. Number of identifiable stages per layer: - KDA layer: 1. RMSNorm x → xn (needs full x of layer input; x is 2304 bf16, tiny — every CTA can compute rmsnorm redundantly? RMSNorm requires a full reduction over 2304 elements. Each CTA could recompute it: read x (4.6KB) per CTA, reduce, multiply by w. That's cheap and avoids barriers! x fits in SMEM/regs easily. 2304 floats = 9KB fp32. Feasible: load x (bf16 4.6KB), compute sumsq via block reduction, then all threads have xn. Or precompute once in stage 0, one barrier. 2. q,k,v,g GEMV = a fused GEMV xn(2304) @ W(2304, 4×4096=16384) → qkvg (16384). One stage: parallel over output columns; each column reads one int4 column (2304/2 bytes) + 18 s/z. Or better, each CTA handles a chunk of columns, loops over k in tiles, vectorized loads. 3. conv + silu on q,k,v: elementwise over 4096 channels, needs the conv window state per channel (3,4096) and conv weights (4 per channel per matrix). Cheap. Can fuse into stage 2's epilogue? The conv needs the FULL q vector? No—conv is depthwise per channel over time dimension of size 4: out[c] = w0*prev2[c] + w1*prev1[c] + w2*prev0[c] + w3*val[c]. Purely per-channel. So it can be computed in the epilogue of the GEMV stage per output element q[c]! And the window update also per channel. And silu is per element. So stage 2 can directly output conv'd q,k,v... but wait g doesn't get conv. And then the KDA recurrence needs per-head q,k,v,g. 4. KDA recurrence: per head h: read S[h] (128×128 fp32 = 64KB), update, write back. 32 heads → distribute across CTAs: 32 CTAs each handle one head, or split. Needs q,k,v,g of that head + beta[h]. beta GEMV (2304×32) tiny. We can fuse beta into stage 2 as 32 extra outputs. 5. o_proj GEMV: o(4096) @ (4096,2304) → attention out, then residual add → h_new. Also write h. 6. RMSNorm h → hm. 7. MoE router: 2304×64 GEMV → softmax → top8. Tiny; one CTA could do it (or all redundantly). 8. Expert GEMVs: for each selected expert e: gate: hm @ (2304,1024) → 1024; up: same; then silu(gate)*up → hidden_state m; down: (1024) @ (1024,2304). 8 routed + 1 shared = 9 experts × 3 GEMVs. 9. combine: out = sum_j w_j down_j + shared; residual. MLA layer similar with rope and attention. The grid-wide dependency chain means maybe ~8-10 barriers per KDA layer, ~10 per MLA layer, total ~40 barriers. Grid-wide barriers cost ~2-5µs each... 40×3µs = 120µs — too much! Need to reduce barrier count and barrier cost. Alternative approach: **fuse much more aggressively with fewer sync points**, and make sync cheap. Actually a grid barrier with atomic counter + spin can be ~1-2µs; total maybe 50-100µs overhead vs 200µs of work. That halves our efficiency. Hmm. Alternative: have every CTA redundantly compute the small ops (rmsnorm inputs small enough) to eliminate barriers. Key insight: the tiny tensors (x 2304, intermediate activations ≤ 16384) are small. The expensive part is reading weights. The barrier structure can be greatly reduced by **fusing stages via redundant computation**: Per layer: - Stage A (GEMV): fused [rmsnorm redundant per CTA... wait RMSNorm needs full x which requires the previous layer's output to be complete. So there's a hard barrier between layers. All reductions over full vectors must complete before the next GEMV... but actually each GEMV column only needs the full input vector x, which is only 2304 elements = 4.6KB. If stage A completes writing x_layer_input (or h, the residual stream), then... Let me restructure with minimal barriers: **Within one KDA layer:** 1. (input: h, the residual of 2304 bf16 which all CTAs can hold). All CTAs compute xn = rmsnorm(h, attn_norm) locally (barrier-free since h is final from previous barrier: h is written at end of previous layer; barrier between layers ensures completion). 2. GEMV1: [q,k,v,g,beta] = xn @ W1 (2304 → 16384+32). Parallel across CTAs over the 16416 outputs. Each CTA computes its slice, applies conv+silu for q,k,v channels in its slice (needs conv state per channel — fine, and updates it), then stores conv'd q,k,v and raw g, beta to a global scratch. Also needs... the KDA recurrence needs full per-head q,k,v,g; that's fine after barrier. 3. GEMV1b / KDA-update: per head: load S[h] (64KB), q,k,v,g[h], beta[h], do update, write S'[h], compute o[h] (128). Parallel over 32 heads (or split columns of S). Wait — but correctness: reference computes S*g.exp() then pred then S += ...; then o = S'^T q where S' is UPDATED S. Yes o uses updated S. Output o (4096) written to scratch. Barrier. 4. GEMV2: o_proj: o(4096) @ (4096,2304) → t; h' = h + t. Writes h' (2304). Barrier... wait residual: h' = x + attn_out. Each output element j: h'[j] = x[j] + t[j]. Fine, elementwise per output column. Write h'. 5. Router: needs h' complete → barrier after 4. All CTAs compute hm = rmsnorm(h', moe_norm) locally; router logits = hm @ W_router (2304×64): each CTA could redundantly compute all 64 logits?? That's 2304×64 = 147K MACs per CTA ×100+ CTAs — wasteful but trivial (0.3 MFLOP). Actually reading the bf16 router weight is 295KB per CTA — every CTA reads it → 100×295KB = 30MB traffic. Too much. Alternative: dedicate the router computation to... hmm. Option: pick ONE CTA (or a few) to do router+topk while others wait at barrier; or precompute top8 indices in the same stage as GEMV2 epilogue: the CTA that computes the last element... complicated. Simple: stage where CTA0 computes router logits + softmax + top8, writes expert ids + weights; other CTAs skip to barrier. That stage is ~150µs?? No: 2304×64 MACs = 147K MACs, one CTA does this in ~10µs at worst? A CTA with 1024 threads doing 147K MACs: each thread ~144 MACs + weight reads 295KB/CTA. One CTA reading 295KB from DRAM ≈ 295KB/(~30GB/s per SM) ≈ 10µs. Hmm, that's slow-ish but acceptable if overlapped. Alternatively compute router logits distributed: "GEMV over 64 outputs" — each of 64 CTAs computes 1 logit (2304 MACs each, reads 9.2KB weight) = <1µs, then one more stage for softmax+topk (one CTA). Then experts stage. Actually, we can avoid the separate softmax+topk stage: distribute finishing: the small ops can be done by CTA0 between barriers; 64-CTA logit stage + CTA0 topk stage = 2 barriers. Or: let ALL CTAs redundantly read logits (64 floats computed in prev stage), each computes softmax+top8 locally (identical results); that eliminates the topk stage — CTAs compute top8 redundantly (deterministic, same fp results). Then expert GEMV stage: work partitioned across CTAs by expert columns (gate/up fused? gate and up both read x (2304) and produce 1024 each; fuse: output 2048 per expert, then silu-mul → m(1024) needs gate+up at same index — same CTA computes column j of gate and j of up → m[j] = silu(g[j])*u[j]. One stage gives m per expert. But down GEMV needs FULL m of that expert → barrier between. down: m(1024) @ (1024,2304) → out contribution. Weighted sum across experts of 2304-vector: could accumulate via atomics? or each expert's down output written separately (9×2304) then one tiny stage to combine with residual. Let me now also double check the earlier traffic estimate for OUR solution; also consider CUDA vs Triton. **Critical design decision: CUDA (load_inline) vs Triton.** Requirements: single launch, grid-wide barriers, persistent CTAs, fine control. CUDA gives us cooperative grid sync if launched via cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel — but with load_inline we can launch with cooperative groups... Actually implementing grid sync manually with atomics is also standard. CUDA gives: - Full control over vectorized loads (e.g., `int4` loads of 16B), __ldg, async copies, smem. - `__syncthreads`, shared memory, reductions, math. - Grid barrier: classic two-phase atomic counter barrier (arrive + spin on generation counter). Well-known pattern. Triton: no explicit grid sync primitive, but you can emulate with atomic_add + spin loop on a scratch counter... Triton does expose `tl.atomic_add` and you can write while loops with `tl.load(..., volatile)`? It's hacky: `while tl.atomic_add(...)` inside @triton.jit — spin loops in Triton are possible (used in some persistent kernel impls) but fragile (compiler may hoist loads; need volatile loads via `tl.load(ptr, volatile=True)` — supported I believe). Also all the tiny-control-flow ops (rope, softmax topk, conv) are fine in Triton. But grid-wide sync in Triton is risky w.r.t. memory ordering; CUDA is much more natural for this megakernel. Also the KDA state update with S in registers/SMEM per head is very CUDA-friendly. I'll go with **CUDA via torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline**, launched once per step from a C++ op. Actually, launch once per step: the Python `step()` calls a custom op e.g. `ext.step(...)` which launches exactly one kernel (cudaLaunchKernel / cooperative). CPU-side overhead ~5-20µs per call; overlapped with GPU so fine. Wait, but is the CPU dispatch + Python the bottleneck? benchmark measures wall time for `steps` iterations of model.step; if GPU kernel takes 250µs, CPU launch (~10-30µs) overlaps → fine. Also need buffers/scratch: preallocate scratch tensors once (persistent across steps, not returned) — allowed: "Internal scratch/workspace may be reused only when it is not returned, does not share storage with returned outputs, and is fully overwritten on every call." The hidden output: we must return a tensor — allocate a fresh output tensor each step (or write into a buffer then clone()... clone launches a kernel! That's an extra launch... the rule says the timed path must be a single kernel launch; a tiny `out = buf.clone()` would be a second kernel. Hmm. But we can allocate an empty tensor (no kernel) and write into it from the megakernel: `out = torch.empty(2304)` each call — allocation via caching allocator ~no kernel launch, then kernel writes into it directly. State: must return updated state. The input state dicts: check.py compares st_sol after step. Can we update state in-place? The input `state` is a list of dicts of tensors — reference `step` mutates st in-place anyway for KDA (st["S"] = S reassigns the dict entry to a new tensor; the original st dict gets updated). For MLA, st["c_kv"] = torch.cat(...) creates a NEW tensor — the reference replaces it. check.py uses whatever st_sol dict contains after step. So we can do the same: preallocate cache with extra capacity? Careful: init_state creates c_kv with exactly context_len rows. step() appends → new tensor with L+1 rows. Each subsequent step grows. In benchmark, `_time` runs `steps` (16-32) steps from the same initial state — each step appends. So per step, cache tensors are recreated with size L+1. For us: preallocate max-size cache buffers? We'd need `c_kv` to be a tensor of shape (L_new, 512) that we return. We could allocate a fresh (L_new,512) tensor per step (empty_like allocation, no kernel) and have the megakernel write... but that requires copying the old cache (L×512×2 bytes = 16.8MB at 16k) — extra traffic per step. Alternatively keep a persistent big buffer of shape (context_len+steps_max, ...) and return a VIEW `buf[:L_new]`. Is returning a view of persistent buffer OK? "Do not return views of those buffers" refers to reused output cache buffers that get overwritten by later calls — a cache buffer that only grows (append-only) and is itself the state... The state persistence rule says don't return views of "reused output cache/scratch buffers" where later calls overwrite earlier outputs. Our cache IS the state — the checker compares st_sol after the step; later steps would append more rows but not touch the first L+1 rows... except benchmark only checks correctness on ONE step from a fresh init_state, and check.py likewise. But the "output-persistence checks" in the harness may check that returned outputs remain stable after subsequent calls. Rows 0..L of the returned view never change afterwards (append-only). But is a VIEW of a persistent buffer flagged? The contract says outputs must be persistent — a view of an append-only buffer is semantically persistent for its lifetime... but strictly, the harness rule: "Do not return `self._out` ... reused output cache/scratch buffers; this is prohibited even if the buffer is fully recomputed on every call, because later calls can overwrite earlier results." Rows aren't overwritten, they're appended. Risky but I think acceptable? Hmm. We'll tune. **Barrier**: classic sense-reversing centralized barrier: ``` __device__ void gbar(volatile unsigned* count, volatile unsigned* gen, int n) { __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x == 0) { unsigned g = *gen; unsigned arrived = atomicAdd((unsigned*)count, 1) + 1; if (arrived == n) { *count = 0; __threadfence(); *gen = g + 1; } else { while (*gen == g) { __nanosleep(64);} } } __syncthreads(); } ``` Hmm, careful with memory ordering: need __threadfence() before atomicAdd (release) and after spin (acquire). Arrival: `__threadfence(); atomicAdd(...)`; releaser: when last arrives: fences then bumps gen. Spinners read gen volatile (L2) — volatile reads bypass L1? On sm_70+, volatile loads go to L2 (ld.volatile.global). Since writes are via volatile store or st.global with threadfence... Standard implementation works (like cooperative groups grid sync fallback). Use `asm volatile("ld.global.cg..." )`? volatile is fine. Note: count reset: `*count = 0` before bumping gen — race: another CTA might still be reading count? No—only arrival touches count, and all arrived by then. But resetting count via plain store while spinning others watch gen — fine. Actually there's a subtle race: next barrier's arrivals (atomicAdd on count) could occur before reset (if some CTA raced ahead into next barrier after seeing gen bump... it will atomicAdd AFTER seeing gen bump; the last arriver does `*count=0; fence; *gen=g+1` — a fast CTA could do atomicAdd for barrier i+1 BEFORE the reset lands? The fast CTA's atomicAdd happens after reading gen==g+1, which happens after the store count=0 is visible IF fences order them: last arriver: `st count=0; __threadfence(); st gen=g+1` — the fence orders the two stores in device-memory visibility. The fast CTA reads gen (acquire: after reading gen bump, does __threadfence() then atomicAdd count). If visibility ordering holds (fence-release / fence-acquire pattern), count=0 store is visible before gen store → fast CTA's add sees count correctly. This is the standard pattern. Good. Modern GPUs do same-address atomics at high throughput: atomicAdd on one address: fire-and-forget except the RETURNING one needs latency. Need to think about reducing barrier cost itself. **Fast barrier design**: - Use multiblock sync via atomic on GLOBAL with L2 (~200ns latency). Cost components: (a) all-to-one atomicAdd: if 376 CTAs add to same counter → serialization at one L2 slice: same-address atomicAdd throughput ~ 1/4ns?? (b) release: one CTA writes flag; others poll: polling with backoff ~ adds latency ≤ poll interval ~ 200-500ns. - Tree barrier: CTAs atomicAdd to per-group counters (e.g. 8 counters of 47), last of each group adds to root → latency ~2× atomic round trip ≈ 600ns + spin. Faster but more code. - Reduce CTA count: 188 CTAs instead of 376 → fewer arrivals. Alternatively **avoid global barrier for small stages via redundant computation** (the R2 pattern) and via **producer-consumer flags** per data item instead of full barrier: e.g., S2 needs qs/ks/vs/g[h] for its head: those are produced by ~16 S1 items (512 outputs / 32-cols per item). Fine-grained flags with spin = deadlock risk if producer CTA hasn't been SCHEDULED... in persistent kernel all CTAs resident; producer will run. But work assignment order matters; if CTA x spins waiting for item owned by CTA y which itself spins waiting for CTA x → deadlock. With STATIC work partition + acyclic stage order, a CTA can produce its S1 items then spin for others' flags — no circular wait IF flags only point backward (S2 waits on S1 flags; S1 items never wait). Deadlock-free. This is "software pipelined stages" — complex; KEEP AS BACKUP optimization. Structure: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_bf16.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> using bf16 = __nv_bfloat16; #define NTHR 256 #define NWARP 8 struct Params { ... ~180 pointers + dims }; __device__ __forceinline__ float bf2f(bf16 v){ return __bfloat162float(v); } ... // grid barrier __device__ void gbar(uint* cnt, uint* gen) { __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x == 0) { __threadfence(); uint g = *((volatile uint*)gen); uint a = atomicAdd(cnt, 1u) + 1; if (a == gridDim.x) { *cnt = 0; __threadfence(); *((volatile uint*)gen) = g + 1; } else { while (*((volatile uint*)gen) == g) { __nanosleep(64); } } __threadfence(); } __syncthreads(); } ``` Hmm wait — issue: `*cnt = 0` via plain store AFTER all arrivals, but OTHER barrier arrivals for NEXT barrier can atomicAdd before the reset is visible?? Discussed: release/acquire ordering via fences: last arriver: store cnt=0; fence; store gen=g+1. A fast CTA spinning reads gen, sees g+1, executes __threadfence(), then (next barrier) atomicAdd cnt. Visibility: gen store visible ⇒ cnt store visible (fence between, and acquire fence after read). CUDA memory model: threadfence = seq per-thread ordering at device scope; paired with the atomic RMW this forms release/acquire ✓ standard. BUT `*cnt = 0` plain store vs other CTA's atomicAdd — atomics bypass L1? Global atomics operate in L2 ✓; plain stores go through L1... `*cnt` plain store hits the CTAs L1 → store to L2 eventually (write-through for global stores? global stores are write-back L2 with L1 write-through on modern archs: stores go to L2 with threadfence pushing). To be safe: use atomicExch(cnt, 0) or store volatile + threadfence: use `atomicExch(cnt,0); __threadfence(); atomicExch(gen, g+1);` — simpler & fully safe. ✓ spitball cost fine. Actually cleaner: single 64-bit counter trick: arrivals atomicAdd((unsigned long long*)cnt, 1); the gen encoded... nah keep 2-word version with atomics. For multiple barriers back-to-back: works (gen increments monotonically). DEADLOCK SAFETY: grid must be ≤ max co-resident. Compute grid = min(114×occ, items_needed...) — occupancy via API on host. All CTAs must be resident before ANY arrives — given grid ≤ capacity ✓ BUT ALSO: barriers require ALL CTAs eventually arrive: work loops must not have ANY CTA exit early!! Every CTA runs the FULL stage sequence even if it has zero items in a stage ✓ code structure guarantees (barrier called unconditionally). **Work distribution patterns per stage (for load balance)**: static: `for (item = blockIdx.x; item < nitems; item += gridDim.x)` — round-robin ✓. **GEMV core** — the critical function. CTA Y spins: sees gen==1 → proceeds. Y reaches barrier 2 quickly and atomicAdds cnt → **but Y's add might happen BEFORE X's `atomicExch(cnt, 0)` is globally visible!** If that happens, Y's atomicAdd reads cnt AFTER X zeroed... ordering with fences: X: atomicExch(cnt, 0); __threadfence(); atomicExch(gen, g+1). Y: reads gen == g+1 (sees the release); __threadfence(); atomicAdd(cnt,1). The fence-release by X orders cnt-store BEFORE gen-store. Y's fence after reading gen acquires: is Y's add guaranteed to see cnt==0? In the CUDA memory model (scoped), the pattern release/acquire via __threadfence + RMW atomics provides ordering IF the gen exchange is observed — the standard pattern (this is exactly how cooperative_groups grid sync is implemented in pre-volta fallbacks...). Actually cooperative groups grid sync uses arrive-wait barriers in a more careful way. The classic Volkov implementation of grid sync: ``` __device__ void sync(volatile unsigned* count, volatile unsigned* gen){ __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x == 0){ unsigned g = *gen; unsigned ticket = atomicAdd((unsigned*)count, 1); if (ticket == gridDim.x - 1){ *count = 0; *gen = g + 1; } else { while (*gen == g) {} } } __syncthreads(); } ``` This is racy the same way in theory but works in practice due to L2 atomics ordering... To be SAFE, the well-known robust version uses the generation embedded in a single counter word, or resets count BEFORE... hmm. Actually the standard safe fix: two counters per used alternately per barrier (ping-pong), and generation flag. Or: arrive with atomicAdd on gen itself: spin until gen == target. AskUserQuestion --add-dir /workspace/problems/02_kda_cutlass -p I need you to implement Kimi Delta Attention forward (chunk form) for the H100 PCIe (SM90 Hopper, HBM2e, 2.0 TB/s). The reference implementation is in reference.py and your kernel goes in solution.py. You can make whatever mess you want in this directory — scratch files, intermediate kernels, build artifacts, profiling traces — but the final answer has to be in solution.py with the same Model, get_inputs, and get_init_inputs interface as reference.py.  The op is the chunk-parallel KDA forward from the FLA library: q and k of shape (B, T, H, K) in bf16, v of shape (B, T, H, V) in bf16, g of shape (B, T, H, K) in fp32 (per-channel log-decay with in-chunk cumsum already applied), beta of shape (B, T, H) in bf16, scale a python float, chunk_size 64, no initial state, no final state. You return o of shape (B, T, H, V) in bf16. Correctness tolerance is 0.05 abs/rel — the long recurrence accumulates more error than a single GEMM so the bar's a bit looser than default bf16. The shapes you have to handle are B=2 T=1024 H=8 K=128 V=128 (short-context training step), B=2 T=2048 H=8 K=128 V=128 (the headline shape from the Kimi Linear paper), B=1 T=4096 H=8 K=128 V=128 (long context that stresses the inter-chunk recurrence), and B=1 T=2048 H=4 K=128 V=128 (thin batch decode).  This needs to be a real custom kernel — the whole point of the problem is to write the chunk-parallel attention yourself, not call FLA's existing implementation. Don't import or call fla.ops.kda, fla.ops.chunk_kda, chunk_kda, fused_recurrent_kda, naive_chunk_kda, or naive_recurrent_kda. The intended path is CUTLASS CuTe on SM90 but Triton, CUDA C++ via load_inline, or inline PTX are also fine if you prefer. Anything you're uncertain about, look up PTX docs, clone CUTLASS or FLA or other reference repos, read library headers, and investigate. AskUserQuestion --add-dir /workspace/problems/03_paged_attention -p I need you to write a paged attention decode kernel for the H100 PCIe (SM90 Hopper, HBM2e, 2.0 TB/s). The reference implementation is in reference.py and your kernel goes in solution.py. You can make whatever mess you want in this directory — scratch files, intermediate kernels, build artifacts, profiling traces — but the final answer has to be in solution.py with the same Model, get_inputs, and get_init_inputs interface as reference.py.  This is single-query decode: each batch element has a query of shape (num_heads, head_dim) and attends over a KV cache of seq_len[b] tokens stored as fixed-size pages in a global pool, with block_table[b] listing which pages belong to batch element b. The KV cache is laid out as (num_blocks, page_size, num_kv_heads, head_dim * 2) where the last dim packs [K | V] so a single gather pulls both. You return the bf16 output of shape (batch, num_heads, head_dim). Correctness tolerance is 0.02 abs/rel on bf16/fp16. The shapes you have to handle are batch=8 num_heads=32 num_kv_heads=8 head_dim=128 seq_len=1024 page_size=16 (Llama-3 8B-style decode), batch=32 num_heads=32 num_kv_heads=8 head_dim=128 seq_len=2048 page_size=16 (server-batched decode), batch=4 num_heads=64 num_kv_heads=8 head_dim=128 seq_len=4096 page_size=16 (Llama-3 70B GQA wide ratio with long context), batch=16 num_heads=32 num_kv_heads=8 head_dim=128 seq_len=1535 page_size=16 (non-power-of-two seq_len that forces predicated tails), and batch=8 num_heads=16 num_kv_heads=4 head_dim=64 seq_len=2000 page_size=16 (small head_dim, non-pow2).  This needs to be a real custom kernel. Don't import or call vllm.attention, flashinfer.batch_decode_with_paged_kv_cache, flashinfer.decode, torch.nn.functional.scaled_dot_product_attention, or F.scaled_dot_product_attention — they're off-limits, including the trick of gathering pages first and then dispatching to SDPA. Try CUDA C++ via load_inline, Triton, inline PTX, or CUTLASS — whatever fits. Anything you're uncertain about, look up PTX docs, clone CUTLASS or vLLM or FlashInfer or other reference repos, read library headers, and investigate.  Your flywheel is implement, profile (ncu, nsys, torch.profiler — whatever's useful) and time it with benchmark.py, verify correctness by running `python check.py` and reading the output, then iterate.
